  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_2ee5fce1-c264-410e-ac0a-a4f95440066f'
      });

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer6150309730511296165">Erich Kästner kennen viele nur als Autor von Kinder- und Jugendbüchern. Dass er auch zeitkritische Romane geschrieben hat, wissen die wenigsten. Der Roman <i>Fabian</i> entstand 1931 und beschreibt den moralischen und geistigen Verfall inmitten schwieriger politischer Verhältnisse. Für Kästner gehen in dieser Zeit politischer und moralischer Verfall der Gesellschaft Hand in Hand.</span>
  <span id="freeText6150309730511296165" style="display:none">Erich Kästner kennen viele nur als Autor von Kinder- und Jugendbüchern. Dass er auch zeitkritische Romane geschrieben hat, wissen die wenigsten. Der Roman <i>Fabian</i> entstand 1931 und beschreibt den moralischen und geistigen Verfall inmitten schwieriger politischer Verhältnisse. Für Kästner gehen in dieser Zeit politischer und moralischer Verfall der Gesellschaft Hand in Hand.<p> Fabian ist ein arbeitsloser Germanist, der durch die Großstadt Berlin zieht auf der Suche nach Arbeit, auf der Suche nach Kontakten. Wir erleben mit, wie er Menschen trifft, die ihn kaufen wollen, wie sich seine Freundin prostituiert, um Schauspielerin zu werden. Sein bester Freund begeht Selbstmord wegen einer lächerlichen Bemerkung. Ein Erfinder tritt auf, der seine Erfindungen, die Arbeitsplätze vernichtet haben, zurücknehmen will, was nicht geht. Die Stadt ist in Auflösung begriffen, die Menschen leben wie in einem Irrenhaus, einem hoffnungslosen, unbarmherzigen Labyrinth.</p><p> Fabian ist ein Außenseiter, ein Moralist, seine Waffe ist die Beobachtung, die Distanz, die durch sarkastische Bemerkungen hergestellt wird. Sein Anspruch besteht darin, zuzusehen, "ob die Welt Talent zur Anständigkeit hat". Kästner stellt dem Moralisten Fabian, ein Mensch übrigens, der moralisch integer ist, ohne dass ihm dies als Lebensentwurf bewusst ist oder er den Zeigefinger erhebt, menschliche Korrumpierbarkeit, Gewissenlosigkeit und Anpassung entgegen. So finden sich im Buch eine ganze Reihe von wirklich gelungenen satirischen Spitzen, wie z.B. eine Tombola, bei der Nahrungsmittel verlost werden oder eine Pöbelei von Menschen als Abendunterhaltung. Kästners Beschreibungen der Menschen sind bissig und schonungslos. Charakteristisch für die Moral der Menschen ist das Verhalten des Redakteurs Müntzer, der etwas unterstützt, an das er nicht glaubt und dessen Falschheit er durchschaut. Von Müntzer heißt es, dass er sein Gewissen chloroformiert hat. </p><p> <i>Fabian</i> ist ein Roman mit vielen wirklich gelungenen zeitkritischen Beobachtungen, die auch heute nichts von ihrer Aktualität verloren haben. Schließlich steht im Zentrum von Kästners Gesellschaftskritik die menschliche Schwäche, die Lethargie, genau um die Falschheit der Verhältnisse zu wissen, aber nichts dagegen zu tun -- und diese Kritik hat auch heute nichts von seiner Berechtigung verloren. <i>--Christoph Steven</i></p></span>
  <a data-text-id="6150309730511296165" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1388421154" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer14795152474904315985"><strong>Es gibt nichts Gutes, außer man tut es</strong><br/>[actions speak louder than words]<br/><br/>I know this book now for more than half of my life. At that time, 1980, when the film came out, our German teacher first let us read the book and then went with us to the movies. A smart move. Incidentally this was the same teacher who also let us read <em>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/494979915' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Catcher in the Rye</a>
</em>. Kästner's Fabian as well as Salinger's Caulfield have become two fictional characters that I probably never will forget.<br/><br/>Now I have learned that ther</span>
  <span id="freeText14795152474904315985" style="display:none"><strong>Es gibt nichts Gutes, außer man tut es</strong><br>[actions speak louder than words]<br><br>I know this book now for more than half of my life. At that time, 1980, when the film came out, our German teacher first let us read the book and then went with us to the movies. A smart move. Incidentally this was the same teacher who also let us read <em>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/494979915" rel="nofollow noopener">The Catcher in the Rye</a>
</em>. Kästner's Fabian as well as Salinger's Caulfield have become two fictional characters that I probably never will forget.<br><br>Now I have learned that there is an uncut and uncensored edition of <em>Fabian</em> available: The 2013 edition from Atrium. More specifically this is the original version that was submitted by Erich Kästner to his publisher. Even the title has been changed according to Kästner's idea: <em>Going to the Dogs</em>. Although the first edition is already very good, I must say that this "naked" version is still a lot sharper. Sharper with regard to the mention of sexual practices and sharper in the political statement.<br><br>The book was written and the story is set in Berlin around 1930. Jakob Fabian is a graduate in German studies, and earns his living as an adman in a cigarette factory (called a "propagandist" in the book). He lives for rent in a single furnished room–money is available but scarce. For certain reasons Fabian loses his job and has to join the vast ranks of the unemployed. Fabian's best and only friend is Stephan Labude, who, after five years, finally submitted his habilitation dissertation about Lessing. Fabian and Labude are doing pub tours, crack jokes, visit music halls. In an artist's studio Fabian meets a young woman, Cornelia Battenberg, and gets to know and finally love her.<br><br>The nice thing about the text is that the author added two postscripts, which say exactly what I would like to say, only much better [translated by me]:<blockquote>
<strong>An die Sittenrichter</strong><br>Dieses Buch ist nichts für Konfirmanden, ganz gleich, wie alt sie sind. Der Autor weist wiederholt auf die anatomische Verschiedenheit der Geschlechter hin. Er läßt in verschiedenen Kapiteln völlig unbekleidete Damen und andere Frauen herumlaufen. Er deutet wiederholt jenen Vorgang an, den man, temperamentloserweise, Beischlaf nennt. Er trägt nicht einmal Bedenken abnorme Spielarten des Geschlechtslebens zu erwähnen. Er unterläßt nichts, was die Sittenrichter zu der Bemerkung veranlassen könnte: Dieser Mensch ist ein Schweinigel.<br>Der Autor erwidert hierauf: Ich bin ein Moralist!<br>[<strong>To the censors</strong><br>This book is not for confirmees, no matter how old they are. The author repeatedly points out the anatomical difference between the sexes. He lets completely naked ladies and other women walk around in different chapters. He insinuates, repeatedly, the process spiritlessly called coitus. He does not even hesitate to mention abnormal varieties of sexual life. He refrains from anything that could cause the censors to remark: This man is a dirty pig.<br>To this the author replies: I am a moralist!]</blockquote><blockquote>
<strong>And die Kunstrichter</strong><br>Die Sittenrichter meinen den Autor, die Kunstrichter meinen das Buch.<br>Dieses Buch nun hat keine Handlung. Außer einer, mit zweihundertsiebzig Mark im Monat dotierten Anstellung geht nichts verloren. Keine Brieftasche, kein Perlenkollier, kein Gedächtnis, oder was sonst im Anfang von Geschichten verloren geht und im letzten Kapitel, zur allgemeinen Befriedigung, wiedergefunden wird. Es wird nichts wiedergefunden. Der Autor hält den Roman keineswegs für eine amorphe Kunstgattung, und trotzdem hat er hier und dieses Mal, die Steine nicht zum Bauen verwandt.<br>Man könnte beinahe vermuten, es handle sich um eine Absicht.<br>[<strong>To the critics</strong><br>The censors mean the author, the critics mean the book.<br>Now this book has no plot. Except for one, an employment endowed with two hundred and seventy marks a month, nothing gets lost. No wallet, no pearl necklace, no memory, or whatever else gets lost in the beginning of stories and is found again, to the general satisfaction, in the last chapter. Nothing is found. The author considers the novel by no means an amorphous genre, and yet he has not used, here and this time, the bricks for building.<br>One might almost suspect that this was done on purpose.]</blockquote><br><br>It is frequently assumed that the author wanted to warn first and above all about the dangers of Nazism and its terrible consequences. While it is true that Nazis repeatedly appear in the book–a magnificent scene includes, for example, the nightly "duel" between a SA-man and a communist activist–, but I think even Kästner didn't have so much of a prophetic gift. I see more of an imminent danger of a <em>civil war</em> and the related total dissolution of civilization. Also the Berlin of the Weimar period, which is alternatively referred to in the book as a madhouse, Sodom and Gomorrah, den of iniquity, or men's brothel reminds me of the decadent Rome shortly before its demise.<br><br>Stylistically, this book is a farce, a caricature. You see the figures often only in a distorting mirror. The dialogs tend to be witty and humorous, but of the dry, sometimes salacious variety. So is the prose. This kind of expression I have never seen elsewhere. You can tell the author has also been a gifted poet. Here are some examples [poorly translated by me]:<br><br>(A man complains that he can no longer meet the needs of his wife)<br><em>Mir wuchs der Unterleib meiner Frau sozusagen über den Kopf.</em><br>[<em>The underbelly of my wife grew over my head, so to speak</em>]<br><br>(Another man bemoans his fate)<br><em>Das einzige, was ich noch nicht erlebt habe, ist der Selbstmord.</em><br>[<em>The only thing I haven't lived through is suicide.</em>]<br><br>(Fabian about the current situation)<br><em>Europa hatte große Pause. Die Lehrer waren fort. Der Stundenplan war verschwunden. Der alte Kontinent würde das Ziel der Klasse nicht erreichen.</em><br>[<em>Europe had long break. The teachers were gone. The timetable was lost. The old continent will not achieve the goal of the class.</em>]<br><br>(Fabian about himself)<br><em>Machthunger und Geldgier sind Geschwister, aber mit mir sind sie nicht verwandt.</em><br>[<em>Lust for power and greed are siblings, but to me they are not related.</em>]<br><br>(A woman who sees herself forced into prostitution)<br><em>Na ja, nun bin ich hier und lebe vom Bauch in den Mund.</em><br>[<em>Well, now I'm here and I live from belly to the mouth.</em>]<br><br>(Fabian's mother to her son)<br><em>[...] du darfst das Leben nicht so schwer nehmen, mein Junge. Es wird dadurch nicht leichter.</em><br>[<em>[...] you must not take life so hard, my boy. It won't become easier through that.</em>]<br><br>(Irene Moll, a recurring character, to Fabian)<br><em>Verhungern ist Geschmacksache.</em><br>[<em>Starving is a matter of taste.</em>]<br><br>(Fabian)<br><em>Daß man lebt, ist Zufall; daß man stirbt, ist gewiß.</em><br>[<em>That you live is by chance; that you die, is certain.</em><br><br>[There are many more examples like these throughout the text]<br><br>When I wrote above about witty and humorous scenes, this is only partly true. There are some–not many–scenes of considerable brutality in this book, and I don't like to talk about those here. In addition this book (in the damned eighteenth chapter) contains a scene which brings me to tears. A tremendously unnecessary injustice! And for the character who caused it I feel an almost physically manifested hatred. I mention this only because I usually do not suffer particularly with the characters of a novel. I am more like a cautious observer, much like Fabian himself in his own way. But chapter eighteen and then later chapter twenty bring me down, they really do.<br><br>In the end I would like to touch on the ending. I realize the ending chosen by Kästner for his novel is certainly not for everyone. I admit it took my a little while to get used to it. Now I think, however, it is perhaps–no definitely–the best ending of a book that I have ever read. In two short sentences (with seven words) Erich Kästner succeeds in summarizing the whole character of his protagonist, and even adds a time-critical foresight for good measure. <br>Well done!<br><br><a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-sa/3.0/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img alt="Creative Commons License" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380148217i/680962.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br>This work is licensed under a <a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by-nc-sa/3.0/" rel="nofollow noopener">Creative Commons Attribution-NonCommercial-ShareAlike 3.0 Unported License</a>.<br></span>
  <a data-text-id="14795152474904315985" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Tony" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33771-tony"><img alt="Tony" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1292869016p2/33771.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3304461564?book_show_action=true">May 04, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tony" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tony" href="/user/show/33771-tony">Tony</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781590175842" href="/book/show/13532203-going-to-the-dogs">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33771-tony?shelf=nyrb-classics">nyrb-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33771-tony?shelf=german">german</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3304461564" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17385650783741413897">This novel had its moments. Like this moment of well-phrased self-reflection:<br/><br/><i>My taste is for blondes, but my experience is against them. I have a preference for tall women. But the attraction is not mutual.</i><br/><br/>And this defense to a broken house rule:<br/><br/><i>It is a well-known fact, madam, that at a certain age desires stir in the human heart that are at variance with the morals of landladies.</i><br/><br/>There was actual fake news when a printer went to his editor to explain there was a blank of five lines on the prepar</span>
  <span id="freeText17385650783741413897" style="display:none">This novel had its moments. Like this moment of well-phrased self-reflection:<br /><br /><i>My taste is for blondes, but my experience is against them. I have a preference for tall women. But the attraction is not mutual.</i><br /><br />And this defense to a broken house rule:<br /><br /><i>It is a well-known fact, madam, that at a certain age desires stir in the human heart that are at variance with the morals of landladies.</i><br /><br />There was actual fake news when a printer went to his editor to explain there was a blank of five lines on the prepared page. <i>'If you need a paragraph and haven't got one, invent one. Watch me!'</i> He quickly dashed off a few lines: <i>'Street fighting between Mohammedans and Hindus has broken out in Calcutta. Although the police soon had the situation in hand the casualties were fourteen dead and twenty-two injured. Order has now been fully restored.'</i> The printer looked up from his reading, <i>'But there's been no fighting in Calcutta. . . . Fourteen dead!'</i> To which the editor responds: <i>'No fighting in Calcutta? . . . Will you kindly prove that? There's always fighting in Calcutta. Are we to report that a sea-serpent has been sighted in the Pacific?'</i><br /><br />And this was a pretty good suicide note: <br /><br /><i>'I thought of telephoning you, but I was too ashamed. I have no talent for receiving sympathy - even at that I'm no good. . . . You would have shown me that my misfortune was microscopically small. I should have appeared to agree with you, and we should each have been deceiving the other. . . .Well, good-bye, Jacob. I had almost written, in all seriousness, I shall often think of you. . . .'</i><br /><br />And yet I was never really fully engaged, which has become an annoying trend (cue the obligatory phrase) in these uncertain times.</span>

<div id="review_1400043526" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1400043526" />
    <a title="notgettingenough " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2658311-notgettingenough"><img alt="notgettingenough " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1258115161p2/2658311.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1400043526?book_show_action=true">Sep 24, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="notgettingenough " class="user" itemprop="url" name="notgettingenough " href="/user/show/2658311-notgettingenough">notgettingenough </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781590175842" href="/book/show/13532203-going-to-the-dogs">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2658311-notgettingenough?shelf=sociology">sociology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2658311-notgettingenough?shelf=modern-lit">modern-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1400043526" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16608975235149142624">My booty on a recent trip to Berlin was more yarn than print, but this was one of the books that made it into my bag for the trip home.<br/><br/>I bought it from St George's English bookshop and if you would like more detail about the wonderful bookshops in Berlin, I wrote something about them <a href='https://cathyingeneva.wordpress.com/2015/09/17/bookshops-in-berlin/' target='_blank' rel='nofollow noopener'>here</a>. <br/><br/>It has a quote on the back from <em>The Times Literary Supplement</em><br/><br/><blockquote>Damned for its improper subject matter, <em>Going to the Dogs </em>showed the crumbling Berlin of Christopher Isherwood's stories with something of Isherwood</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText16608975235149142624" style="display:none">My booty on a recent trip to Berlin was more yarn than print, but this was one of the books that made it into my bag for the trip home.<br /><br />I bought it from St George's English bookshop and if you would like more detail about the wonderful bookshops in Berlin, I wrote something about them <a href="https://cathyingeneva.wordpress.com/2015/09/17/bookshops-in-berlin/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>. <br /><br />It has a quote on the back from <em>The Times Literary Supplement</em><br /><br /><blockquote>Damned for its improper subject matter, <em>Going to the Dogs </em>showed the crumbling Berlin of Christopher Isherwood's stories with something of Isherwood's sharp intelligence, but a far more tragic sense of implication.</blockquote><br /><br />It's a comparison I'm looking forward to making for myself, having acquired the relevant Isherwood volume, also a slim affair, at the same time. One can certainly agree that Kästner has an eerie feel for what is happening in Germany and what the outcome will be, an outcome he knows will be far more widespread. In 1931 he is well aware that he is watching the downfall, the disintegration, the degeneration of Europe. It's horrifying to be aware, reading it now, and seeing the ways in which it compares with Europe now, that there was no hindsight on the author's. He was calling it as he saw it day by day. <br /><br />Rest here:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://alittleteaalittlechat.wordpress.com/2015/09/24/going-to-the-dogs-the-story-of-a-moralist-by-erich-kastner/">https://alittleteaalittlechat.wordpre...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="16608975235149142624" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_464078947" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/464078947" />
    <a title="Nicholas During" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/440220-nicholas-during"><img alt="Nicholas During" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1306953644p2/440220.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/464078947?book_show_action=true">Nov 26, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicholas During" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicholas During" href="/user/show/440220-nicholas-during">Nicholas During</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781590175842" href="/book/show/13532203-going-to-the-dogs">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer464078947" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6716574034023875300">Very reminiscent of Isherwood's Berlin Stories, except this one is much bleaker and from a German. The great thing about this book is it is not really a witness to the rise of Nazism, though it of course is, because it is so absurd, and the narrator is so cynical, while reading it is hard to trust what he (Fabian) is telling us. In fact one almost suspects Fabian himself doesn't believe what he sees and recounts. And who can blame him, Weimar Berlin must have been a pretty surreal place to live.</span>
  <span id="freeText6716574034023875300" style="display:none">Very reminiscent of Isherwood's Berlin Stories, except this one is much bleaker and from a German. The great thing about this book is it is not really a witness to the rise of Nazism, though it of course is, because it is so absurd, and the narrator is so cynical, while reading it is hard to trust what he (Fabian) is telling us. In fact one almost suspects Fabian himself doesn't believe what he sees and recounts. And who can blame him, Weimar Berlin must have been a pretty surreal place to live. And it reminds me of Alexanderplatz in that it is not totally a political novel but one interested in the actions and psychology of the characters.<br /><br />And what are we to make of Fabian? Well, at heart he is a moralist, and proves it in the end. Any reading of this book as a critique of the passivity of German liberalism in the wake of Nazism, and definitely it (like in Isherwood) is here, is totally wrong. Does Fabian know how to fix his country or city? No he bloody well doesn't. Do you? Should he have tried harder? Perhaps, but this book was written in 1931, before the extent of the evil to come was known and the most common "action" among leftist intellectuals like Fabian was probably to flee. Fabian doesn't know what to do, and he sees the evil even in his best friends. (I'm no Arendt expert but could the banality of evil be represented by a young girlfriend sleeping with an ugly old movie producer to get a break and make some dough?). This kind of stuff is depressing. It's one thing to rail against the political powers, another to hear the awful compromising and downright immorality of your friends, colleagues, and age group. And Fabian sees a lot of this everywhere he goes. But though Fabian is a moralist, he is not perfect. And though he might damn those who sell their bodies for money, he isn't much better himself. And knows it.<br /><br />This book does feel like Grosz or Ernst or whatever Neue Sachlichkeit painter is in vogue at the moment. It really is a literary expression of the same thing. Gross but clear. Absurd but honest. Humans doing inhuman things to each other. This book is a true literary classic of the Weimar era. I'm surprised it's not more popular.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6716574034023875300" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1087334432" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1087334432" />
    <a title="German Embassy Book Club" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30567370-german-embassy-book-club"><img alt="German Embassy Book Club" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1397574637p2/30567370.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1087334432?book_show_action=true">Oct 23, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="German Embassy Book Club" class="user" itemprop="url" name="German Embassy Book Club" href="/user/show/30567370-german-embassy-book-club">German Embassy Book Club</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30567370-german-embassy-book-club?shelf=classic-german-literature">classic-german-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30567370-german-embassy-book-club?shelf=erich-k%C3%A4stner-a-german-legend">erich-kästner-a-german-legend</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1087334432" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3694283267568258850">In many ways, “Fabian,” by Erich Kästner, opens like many other 20th century German novels, with a young man not sure about his life. In the 1910s, 20s, and 30s, the ‘bildungsroman’ style was extremely popular across all genres. In this case, the protagonist, Fabian, is a recent college graduate without much direction who finds himself mixed up with the teeming underbelly of Berlin society. From prostitutes to beggars to corrupt politicians, Fabian observes those around him with a degree of dist</span>
  <span id="freeText3694283267568258850" style="display:none">In many ways, “Fabian,” by Erich Kästner, opens like many other 20th century German novels, with a young man not sure about his life. In the 1910s, 20s, and 30s, the ‘bildungsroman’ style was extremely popular across all genres. In this case, the protagonist, Fabian, is a recent college graduate without much direction who finds himself mixed up with the teeming underbelly of Berlin society. From prostitutes to beggars to corrupt politicians, Fabian observes those around him with a degree of distance. He and his equally hopeless friend Labude take nightly trips to the brothels of Berlin to cure Labude’s broken heart. From there, his idea of a moral life is challenged repeatedly, and he must come to terms with his own beliefs. <br />In a bordello one evening, Fabian meets Cornelia Battenberg, a spurned woman who is pessimistic about love. They fall into bed together that night and begin a cautious, but passionate relationship. Only a few days later, when Fabian believes himself to be at the peak of his happiness, he loses his job as a “propagandist” for a tobacco company. What started with unemployment quickly spirals and Fabian’s life soon seems teetering on the edge of hopelessness.<br />On a grander scale, Fabian’s story is a clear attempt to address Germany’s steady decline into National Socialism in the 1930s. Early on in the novel, Kästner includes a fight scene between a Nazi and a communist, which is an allegory for the increased polarization between these two political beliefs in Germany in the 30s. Fabian is constantly confronted with signs of failing social norms: he watches as newspapers print blatant lies daily; is offered a job writing for a right-wing news organization; and sees how the film industry is controlled by those with money and power. Fabian is one of the only society novels of the late 1920s, which makes it unique. It documents the rise of Nazism by describing the decline of empathy and moralism.<br />In order to truly understand Fabian, one must understand Erich Kästner. His children’s books are akin to the Hardy boys or Nancy Drew in the US – everyone read them as a kid, everyone loved them. Some were translated, but their popularity never really took off in the US. Kästner’s novel Fabian is one of the only instances in which Kästner breaks away from children’s literature and writes for adults. Marcel Reich Raniki, one of the greatest German book critics of all time, called Kästner ‘Germany’s most hopeful pessimist,’ because, although he criticized society, Kästner was at heart a lover of the German language. He is one of the only great German authors who stayed in Germany throughout WWII. He watched as his books, including Fabian, were labeled degenerate and burned outside of libraries across the country. He watched the country rebuild itself from the ashes. And still, he stayed. That alone sets him apart from the others and makes “Fabian” especially fascinating.<br />“Fabian” is worth reading for anyone interested in German history. Kästner had a talent for deciphering the tone of a decade, and with Fabian he succinctly synthesized the years leading up to the war. It is a fast read, and one full of symbolism. <br /><br /><b> Review by Lauren Rogers, Press Officer, German Embassy Washington </b></span>
  <a data-text-id="3694283267568258850" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1194784540" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1194784540" />
    <a title="Leah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/39972744-leah"><img alt="Leah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1437513165p2/39972744.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1194784540?book_show_action=true">Feb 08, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leah" href="/user/show/39972744-leah">Leah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39972744-leah?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39972744-leah?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39972744-leah?shelf=german-classics">german-classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1194784540" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer148262408638138729">A great portrait of the city Berlin during the Weimar Republic!<br/><br/>The book is set right before the downfall fo the Weimar Republic, with all the political tensions and the morals slowly collapsing, women who gave up on love and just use sex, men without work, just coffee houses and brothels. And right in the middle, there is Jakob Fabian, an unemployed germanist and moralist.<br/>For me, this book delivers a great and impressive portray of the desperate time after the global economic crisis from 1929. A</span>
  <span id="freeText148262408638138729" style="display:none">A great portrait of the city Berlin during the Weimar Republic!<br /><br />The book is set right before the downfall fo the Weimar Republic, with all the political tensions and the morals slowly collapsing, women who gave up on love and just use sex, men without work, just coffee houses and brothels. And right in the middle, there is Jakob Fabian, an unemployed germanist and moralist.<br />For me, this book delivers a great and impressive portray of the desperate time after the global economic crisis from 1929. All the characters seemed kinda helpless and without perspective. While reading, I started to feel their desperation and could fully understand why extremism grew in this city. I could imagine every monument they passed and after reading had a better impression of Berlin which I last visited in 2009 and never in the 30's, of course.<br />I also loved how women were presented in the book. While at first they seemed strong, confident and liberated, you saw that this was just a mask and in reality they were disappointed by life and men. I felt like Kästner wanted to criticise the way men behaved towards women and that they always underestimated their gender. <br /><br />I especially loved the end and how it all fit together. In addition I liked the style of writing and even in sad occasions, and there are many in this book, I had to laugh because of the sarcastic tone the book is set in. If you love Berlin, I definitly recommend this book.<br /><br />This was my first novel by Kästner that was not written for children. I was really excited before reading it if it would differ from his children books. And I was not disappointed. His work is highly developed in style, sarcastic in tone and criticises the society in a way that makes you laugh at first and then think.</span>
  <a data-text-id="148262408638138729" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1093829842" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1093829842" />
    <a title="Cerisaye" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15718667-cerisaye"><img alt="Cerisaye" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1093829842?book_show_action=true">Oct 31, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cerisaye" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cerisaye" href="/user/show/15718667-cerisaye">Cerisaye</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781590176870" href="/book/show/16030137-going-to-the-dogs">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15718667-cerisaye?shelf=fiction-in-translation-german">fiction-in-translation-german</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15718667-cerisaye?shelf=weimar-germany">weimar-germany</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1093829842" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14837078616824014699">I have been reading German fiction in translation and this novel by the author of <i>Emil and the Detectives</i> appeared on one of those "If you have read this then you might be interested in..." Recommendations pages. Weimar Germany has fascinated me since I saw <i>Cabaret</i> as an impressionable teen in 1974, so I picked up the novel eager for an insider view of the period. I didn't read the introduction until after finishing the book which turned out to be a wise decision because it contains major spoile</span>
  <span id="freeText14837078616824014699" style="display:none">I have been reading German fiction in translation and this novel by the author of <i>Emil and the Detectives</i> appeared on one of those "If you have read this then you might be interested in..." Recommendations pages. Weimar Germany has fascinated me since I saw <i>Cabaret</i> as an impressionable teen in 1974, so I picked up the novel eager for an insider view of the period. I didn't read the introduction until after finishing the book which turned out to be a wise decision because it contains major spoilers, though it has useful background and criticism.<br /><br />Kastner shows the decadence of Weimar Berlin hurtling towards destruction. The novel is suffused with a sense that something awful is about to happen- it was published in 1931 which makes this quote (the context is a dream) chillingly prescient:<br /><br /><i>In front of them towered a machine as vast as Cologne Cathedral. Before it were standing workmen, stripped to the waist. They were armed with shovels, and were shoveling hundreds of thousands of babies into a huge furnace where a red fire was burning.</i><br /><br />Fabian the main character observes what is happening around him as his own life lurches from struggling, as a poorly paid, under-appreciated advertising copywriter, to hopelessness when he is cast into the rapidly swelling ranks of unemployed: German society rewards cynics and opportunists not the decent and kind. He is a likeable character for all his faults, a good friend and dutiful son.<br /><br />Not a lighthearted novel but easy to read and quite funny, though overall quite a sad book. Definitely something to look for if you have any interest in this period.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14837078616824014699" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10148904-lily?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>,

<span id="freeTextContainer113333131031153268">I always intend to read more books that are written by German authors, and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1131999.Fabian__Die_Geschichte_eines_Moralisten' title='Fabian. Die Geschichte eines Moralisten by Erich Kästner' rel='nofollow noopener'>Fabian. Die Geschichte eines Moralisten</a> was one that reinforced that resolution of mine, because it was a highly worthwhile and intellectually rewarding read.<br/><br/>I'm of the opinion that you can tell a lot about an author's outlook on life and intellect by reading their books, and following that line of thought I deduce <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6519.Erich_K_stner' title='Erich Kästner' rel='nofollow noopener'>Erich Kästner</a> to be a person who is not afraid to call a spade a spade, to provoke, or to offend. His portra</span>
  <span id="freeText113333131031153268" style="display:none">I always intend to read more books that are written by German authors, and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1131999.Fabian__Die_Geschichte_eines_Moralisten" title="Fabian. Die Geschichte eines Moralisten by Erich Kästner" rel="nofollow noopener">Fabian. Die Geschichte eines Moralisten</a> was one that reinforced that resolution of mine, because it was a highly worthwhile and intellectually rewarding read.<br /><br />I'm of the opinion that you can tell a lot about an author's outlook on life and intellect by reading their books, and following that line of thought I deduce <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6519.Erich_K_stner" title="Erich Kästner" rel="nofollow noopener">Erich Kästner</a> to be a person who is not afraid to call a spade a spade, to provoke, or to offend. His portrayal of society is honest, blunt, outspoken—and often a bit scandalous, but immensely entertaining.<br /><br />That being said, you have to be in the right mood to read this book, because it's not the easiest to get through, and I had to put it aside more than once. A little warning beforehand: Don't let the ending upset you like it did me.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Giovanna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Giovanna" href="/user/show/65153-giovanna">Giovanna</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/65153-giovanna?shelf=nyrb">nyrb</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1037999980" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer235275876900978122">An unsettling book to read right now. I started it while traveling in Germany--in Dusseldorf, which in 2016 is very different than Berlin of the 20s. But the book feels very familiar, with its chaotic background and Fabian, the narrator, struggling to support himself and find meaning. His rent (for a room in a boarding house) is 40% of his pay, which sounds familiar.<br/><br/>Towards the end, he's back home and runs into an old classmate who's joined up with the Stahlhelm (I think it's a nationalistic pre</span>
  <span id="freeText235275876900978122" style="display:none">An unsettling book to read right now. I started it while traveling in Germany--in Dusseldorf, which in 2016 is very different than Berlin of the 20s. But the book feels very familiar, with its chaotic background and Fabian, the narrator, struggling to support himself and find meaning. His rent (for a room in a boarding house) is 40% of his pay, which sounds familiar.<br /><br />Towards the end, he's back home and runs into an old classmate who's joined up with the Stahlhelm (I think it's a nationalistic precurser to the Nazis). When he tells the friend they will fail and bring down Germany with them, the friend responds, "Then we shall all go under, and to hell with it."<br /><br />Fabian responds, "Where do you get the audacity to condemn 60 million people to ruin, simply because you're fond of a scrap and possess the sense of honor of an insulted turkey-cock?"<br /><br />I sure hope our 2016 turkey-cock gets soundly defeated in three weeks.</span>
  <a data-text-id="235275876900978122" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2848402891" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2848402891" />
    <a title="Barnaby Thieme" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1944947-barnaby-thieme"><img alt="Barnaby Thieme" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1255549111p2/1944947.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2848402891?book_show_action=true">Jun 30, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Barnaby Thieme" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Barnaby Thieme" href="/user/show/1944947-barnaby-thieme">Barnaby Thieme</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9783038820086" href="/book/show/36483221-fabian">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1944947-barnaby-thieme?shelf=literature">literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2848402891" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3511468829956734767">Erich Kästner's "Fabian" is the story of an attractive literary type: a disaffected and somewhat misanthropic intellectual cast adrift of the streets of Weimar-era Berlin. He travels incessantly through the center of the city by foot, by car, and by train, with a carefully-described geographical accuracy that is impressive to anyone who knows the city. That was for me one of the book's special pleasures, living, as I do, within the center of Fabian's stomping ground. <br/><br/>Fabian reminded me a bit of </span>
  <span id="freeText3511468829956734767" style="display:none">Erich Kästner's "Fabian" is the story of an attractive literary type: a disaffected and somewhat misanthropic intellectual cast adrift of the streets of Weimar-era Berlin. He travels incessantly through the center of the city by foot, by car, and by train, with a carefully-described geographical accuracy that is impressive to anyone who knows the city. That was for me one of the book's special pleasures, living, as I do, within the center of Fabian's stomping ground. <br /><br />Fabian reminded me a bit of Stephen Daedelus in Ulysses - over-educated and unter-utilized, adrift in a city that he loves but which has little use for him, cherishing the commonplace and mistrusting the grand, and watching, always watching. <br /><br />But this book is no Ulysses. Kästner is an author who does small things well and big things poorly. Marcel Reich-Ranicki describes him as a "minor master," and warns his readers not to overlook him on that account, for Germany is a land that "loves Bach but undervalues Offenbach," and has little use for minor masters. <br /><br />I appreciated the style and the craft of the prose for the most part, which vividly conveyed impressions and ideas, but I found myself largely unmoved by the larger points the book sought to make, such as they were. Many of the conflicts set up by the book failed to resolve in a way that communicated anything of significance. <br /><br />In particular, the most important action that takes place in the book depends entirely on something done by a character that I found completely unbelievable. No one would do what that character did, and if it was intended as satire, its point was completely opaque to me, other than the rather trivial point that great spirits are often destroyed by the petty actions of bureaucrats. <br /><br />I generally found Kästner's psychology unpersuasive, and I was frequently repelled by his characterizations of women, who are consistently depicted as needy whores, sexually devouring and, inexplicably, prone to devote themselves excessively to the protagonist, even when they barely knew him. <br /><br />A low-point for me was his depiction of a lesbian bar in Berlin, and his obnoxious observation that most lesbians are straight women who are angry with men. One gets the feeling that Kästner knew very little about women, and felt extremely uncomfortable around them. <br /><br />This book is often described as a social satire, but it's hard for me to see it as such - I don't recognize the target of his satire, because I don't recognize the characters or their world as anything actual.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3511468829956734767" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3198888710" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3198888710" />
    <a title="Sarita" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/62680378-sarita"><img alt="Sarita" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505669779p2/62680378.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3198888710?book_show_action=true">Jul 03, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarita" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarita" href="/user/show/62680378-sarita">Sarita</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781590175842" href="/book/show/13532203-going-to-the-dogs">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/62680378-sarita?shelf=germany">germany</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3198888710" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16986891914060604299">Going to the Dogs by Erich Kästner tells a story about Jakob Fabian, a moralist living in Berlin after the crash of 1929 before the Nazi takeover. He is a 32 year old with excellent education but permanently condemned to a low-paid job without security. He finds himself stuck in the rising of unemployment and spiritual depression of the country.<br/><br/>Despite the hopeless situation, Fabian and friends make the best of it. Some go to work although they can get laid off at any moment (someone got laid of</span>
  <span id="freeText16986891914060604299" style="display:none">Going to the Dogs by Erich Kästner tells a story about Jakob Fabian, a moralist living in Berlin after the crash of 1929 before the Nazi takeover. He is a 32 year old with excellent education but permanently condemned to a low-paid job without security. He finds himself stuck in the rising of unemployment and spiritual depression of the country.<br /><br />Despite the hopeless situation, Fabian and friends make the best of it. Some go to work although they can get laid off at any moment (someone got laid off because he’s too qualified; too expensive) and some continue studying despite the lack of employment. They enjoy cabarets and plays in the evenings while making sharp observations on politics, life, and love.<br /><blockquote>Labude shook his head. ‘That is inertia. If you earn money and don’t want it, you can exchange it for power.’<br />‘And what should I do with power?’ asked Fabian. ‘I know you want power. But what should I do with power, when I don’t want to be powerful? The love of power and the love of money are members of one family, but it’s not my family.’<br />‘You can use power for the good of others.’<br />‘But who does? One man uses it for his own advantage, another for that of his family, one for his fellow supertax-payers, another for people with fair hair, a fifth for those over six feet tall, a sixth in order to test some mathematical formula on humanity. To hell with money and power!’</blockquote><br /><br />This is a satirical work depicting life in the big city. It does not describe what things were like; it exaggerates them. It shows how people keep losing their job to new technology, businessman keep busy making money and anyone else goes to dance/sex clubs, and how some maintain courage and virtues through it all.<br /><br /><blockquote>There were flowers on the table, and a letter. He opened it. A twenty-mark note dropped out, and a scrap of paper. ‘A little present. Love, Mother,’ was written on it. … He put the twenty-mark note in his pocket.<br /><br />There was his mother in the train; very soon she would find the twenty-mark note he had slipped into her handbag. Mathematically considered, the result equaled nothing. For both were now as poor as they had been before. But good deeds are beyond book-keeping. The moral and the arithmetical equations work out differently.</blockquote><br /><br />Written in 1931, the idea is still very relevant today. The moralist is a great reminder not to get discouraged with the state of the world that can seem hopeless at times and to keep doing the right thing even if it doesn’t seem to make any difference.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16986891914060604299" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1788604495" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1788604495" />
    <a title="Pascale" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17430783-pascale"><img alt="Pascale" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1363794428p2/17430783.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1788604495?book_show_action=true">Oct 19, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pascale" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pascale" href="/user/show/17430783-pascale">Pascale</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781590175842" href="/book/show/13532203-going-to-the-dogs">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1788604495" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer596333606043310428">Not quite as powerful as the best of Irmgard Keun, but very strong nonetheless. Fabian, a bright guy from a modest background, makes a living devising advertising campaigns for a cigarette company. His best friend is Labude, a man from a much wealthier family who is anxiously awaiting his supervisor's verdict on this PhD on Lessing. Both men stroll through the streets of Berlin, repulsed by the rampant immorality they see around them. Early on, a woman named Irene Moll drags Fabian back to her p</span>
  <span id="freeText596333606043310428" style="display:none">Not quite as powerful as the best of Irmgard Keun, but very strong nonetheless. Fabian, a bright guy from a modest background, makes a living devising advertising campaigns for a cigarette company. His best friend is Labude, a man from a much wealthier family who is anxiously awaiting his supervisor's verdict on this PhD on Lessing. Both men stroll through the streets of Berlin, repulsed by the rampant immorality they see around them. Early on, a woman named Irene Moll drags Fabian back to her place to have sex in front of her husband. The couple has a kind of contract, and the husband is prepared to pay Fabian for his services. Fabian declines politely but keeps the keys to the apartment out of carelessness since he has no intention of ever using them. Irene crosses paths with Fabian several more times. After her husband is convicted of embezzlement, she opens a brothel for female customers and offers Fabian, by then unemployed, a job as her secretary. In their final encounter she tries, again in vain, to convince him to emigrate with her. Fabian turns her down, foreshadowing Kästner's own attitude a few years later. It is typical of Fabian's stance throughout the novel that while wanting no part of Irene's lifestyle, he is not outraged by her behavior the way Labude is. Labude is a much more intense sort of guy, who wants to believe there is still room for democracy and political change in the increasingly shaky Weimar Republic. Fabian only starts to want to be more active in the world when he falls in love with Cornelia, a young woman who has been badly betrayed by a previous lover and initially seems to blossom in her relationship with sweet and sincere Fabian. However, when Fabian loses his job, Cornelia decides to move on and becomes the mistress of a film producer to advance her career. Simultaneously, Fabian is dealt another blow when Labude, also unhappy in love, but above all disappointed in his academic ambitions, commits suicide. It turns out that out of malice Labude's supervisor's assistant had told Labude that his thesis had been rejected, when on the contrary the professor was about to to award him the highest honors. Demoralized by Labude's senseless death, the result of a mean hoax, Fabian retreats to his home town and his beloved mother. There he easily finds an ill-paid job, which however would be sufficient to keep him afloat since he has inherited some money from Labude. But without Cornelia at his side, Fabian doesn't see the point of slaving away to enrich shareholders. Instead, he fantasizes about trying a hermit's life in the countryside: "But was not this plan of his a mere escape? Was there not always, everywhere, a stage for those who wished to act? For what had he been waiting all these years? Perhaps for the realization that he had been born and ordained a spectator, and not, as he still believed, an actor in the world's theatre." Shortly after he's had this insight into his own character, he sees a little boy fall into the river. Instinctively, he dives in: "The little boy swam ashore, howling. Fabian sank. Unfortunately he could not swim." What a beautifully economical ending! This has to be one of the best books published in Germany between the wars, and I hope it will never be out of print again.</span>
  <a data-text-id="596333606043310428" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText13396475539917522327" style="display:none">It's counter-intuitive, but I'd have to say that if you're coming into this novel with a strong foundation in the arts and letters of time (hem-burning pre-Nazi cabaret scum booze Grosz anatomies--or, you know who Anita Berber is and can imitate her at length), then you probably might find this novel a little redundant as I did.<br />Yes, yes, there's all the angst and weirdness of the on-the-Nazi-cusp, the fever and fervor of inter-war Berlin, of an entire culture on the brink. It's funny, too, in a sad, pale transvestite spitting olives into a chimp's mouth kind of way. But fans of this kind of thing might find it a little overwrought or a little over-indulgent.<br />People who have no idea what I'm talking about, however, will probably benefit from coming to the fascinating and grim deviant-laden inter-war period via this book, so you people should read it before I get a midget to shit in your shoes.</span>

Although it is not a lighthearted book, "Fabian" is very smart and gives you an insight into this generation and the problems they faced. </span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2450566380&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2450566380" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2824272946075788550"><strong>Candid(e) look at Weimar Berlin</strong><br/><br/>Originally drawn to the his ironic poetry, I was unaware of this, probably his most widely read, novel. A novel which originally was subjected to censorship and later outright banned and burned under the Nazis. The novel reads quickly as it provides a glimpse of the Weimar nightlife with a memorable cast of characters and shocking situational humor. Few characters are spared their own self-compromising descent into corrupting their moral image, and as their self-wo</span>
  <span id="freeText2824272946075788550" style="display:none"><strong>Candid(e) look at Weimar Berlin</strong><br /><br />Originally drawn to the his ironic poetry, I was unaware of this, probably his most widely read, novel. A novel which originally was subjected to censorship and later outright banned and burned under the Nazis. The novel reads quickly as it provides a glimpse of the Weimar nightlife with a memorable cast of characters and shocking situational humor. Few characters are spared their own self-compromising descent into corrupting their moral image, and as their self-worth corrodes, Fromm‘s fear of freedom looms ever larger - as it is writ, the self is a commodity that at this time most cannot afford, and so the responsibility of this freedom - for the conscientious - destroys them.<br /><br />For me, the most striking and memorable passage appears in chapter XVII as the previous action is summarized in a metaphor of consumption. Fabian’s dream imitates or is at least inspired by the avant Garde art of the time (much like Kafka’s dream in Amerika or Lang’s Moloch vision in Metropolis). For example, Frau Moll (who has become a purveyor of young male escorts) is dipping her hand into a popcorn bucket, but instead of popped kernels, she is lifting miniature young men, undressing them and then popping them into her mouth. The imagery epitomizes the hopelessness in which they all (including Fabian) have come to Berlin in search for another avenue of stimulation, and that with all of this, their desire is nothing more than an empty, unsatisfying repetitive refusal to lead a reflective life - to participate in life in a socially productive way. Instead, what they delude themselves with is an escape from life, even Fabian’s attempt to rescue the drowning boy, is like what so many of the characters seek, an escape from the freedom of being responsible for oneself by diving into distraction from one’s own troubles.<br /> <br />Apart from the symbolic chapter, a more apt comparison I come to think of is that of Voltaire’s “Candide” and rather than a naive optimist, we have a rather self-corrupting ‘moralistic’ pessimist. Fabian indulges himself, for lack of something better to do, while summarily rejecting the lifestyle. He is a moralist, but one without the resolve to commit in any way, which eventually he leaves the city to try and find peace at home. This is perhaps why some of his poetry alludes to his own self-recrimination for the instinct of self-preservation and especially for sitting on the fence, while the world transforms into the national nightmare before him. <br /><br />Ultimately, I think the book is intentionally sensationalistic (it’ll sell better - as Fabian and the author are very aware of impactful advertising) and at the same time, it is rather honest in its depiction of Berlin nightlife and the characters that populate the novel have counterparts in Kaestner’s biography. Unfortunately, I can also see how it contributes - through the stylizing of immorality and at the same time undermining it’s liberating qualities with moralist undercurrents - to a rejection of the freedom of responsibility that Fromm saw as contributing to the rise of the Nazis. We are left with the abrupt demise that surely Kaestner, as an educator, lives metaphorically through Fabian, and acts quickly to try to rescue the drowning boy, who has been swept up by the cultural current. The novel leaves us with the message that to attempt to rescue the youth is folly, either they find their own way out, or you may yourself be in jeopardy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2824272946075788550" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3556854873" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3556854873" />
    <a title="Dan Keefer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/40301777-dan-keefer"><img alt="Dan Keefer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1424014864p2/40301777.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3556854873?book_show_action=true">Sep 19, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dan Keefer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dan Keefer" href="/user/show/40301777-dan-keefer">Dan Keefer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781590175842" href="/book/show/13532203-going-to-the-dogs">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3556854873" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1018446175788481094">Weimar Republic. Berlin, Germany. late 1920s to early 1930s.<br/><br/>Kästner IS a moralist and satirizes the beer and sex "depravity" of the down and out Berliners of the "Cabaret" period. Fabian and Labude are old college buddies who in their 30s become victims of the Great Depression and the Versailles Treaty. Their lives consist of low-rent apartments they sometimes can't afford and good-hearted prostitutes, who occasionally will have sex with them for free because business is slow and they (the prost</span>
  <span id="freeText1018446175788481094" style="display:none">Weimar Republic. Berlin, Germany. late 1920s to early 1930s.<br /><br />Kästner IS a moralist and satirizes the beer and sex "depravity" of the down and out Berliners of the "Cabaret" period. Fabian and Labude are old college buddies who in their 30s become victims of the Great Depression and the Versailles Treaty. Their lives consist of low-rent apartments they sometimes can't afford and good-hearted prostitutes, who occasionally will have sex with them for free because business is slow and they (the prostitutes) craved sex all of the time. (Of course, this was a time when respectable women hid their sexual appetites lest the be treated as tramps.) <br /><br />The author calls Fabian a "moralist" ironically because Fabian. in his eyes, presents the decaying of German society.<br /><br />But the novel is heralded because it so accurately portrays what life was for the impoverished during that period of history. The author postulates that there is no value to living under good conditions if you are not also good. For me, he misses the mark whenever he becomes a philosopher and social commentator. It seems rather that the real lesson is what happens to good people who are crushed by bad conditions. Fabian and Labude try to be good people and largely are. <br /><br />Of course, times have changed and American society is different from German society. Perhaps Kästner's contemporaries by-and-large saw the same world that he saw. <br /><br />None of this takes away from the enjoyment and thought-provoking ideas presented in this novel. <br />The characters are believable and mostly likeable, and the story (I wouldn't call it a plot) moves in a way that keeps the reader engaged. With only 178 pages, it's not a huge commitment. So read it!</span>
  <a data-text-id="1018446175788481094" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3615995369" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3615995369" />
    <a title="Timo Bremer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/50064390-timo-bremer"><img alt="Timo Bremer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1450012823p2/50064390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3615995369?book_show_action=true">Oct 27, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Timo Bremer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Timo Bremer" href="/user/show/50064390-timo-bremer">Timo Bremer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3615995369" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11999292547434245613">This was written about the Berlin of the late 1920s, but it remains an extremely accurate portrayal of Berlin and so many other major cities today - of the world that young men and women can find themselves in right after college. Thesis writing, intergenerational conflict between milennials and boomers, unemployment, sexual and relationship mayhem, the hopelessness with regards to family formation, the corrupted or meaningless nature of the jobs that you can actually get, the moral bankruptcy o</span>
  <span id="freeText11999292547434245613" style="display:none">This was written about the Berlin of the late 1920s, but it remains an extremely accurate portrayal of Berlin and so many other major cities today - of the world that young men and women can find themselves in right after college. Thesis writing, intergenerational conflict between milennials and boomers, unemployment, sexual and relationship mayhem, the hopelessness with regards to family formation, the corrupted or meaningless nature of the jobs that you can actually get, the moral bankruptcy of older superiors, moving back in with your parents, the wrottenness of the whole thing and the suffering it all causes - it's spot on across the board. From that perspective it's an excellent book.<br /><br />The big 'but' is that it stops at that. It shows you with brutal honesty how bad things are, but it never goes beyond that. It is nothing but a big complaint about the current state of things. There is no stoicism, christianity, political ideology or any other attempt to find a way to make things better. I understand that that is how the main character felt and how many people feel and that leaving it where the author left it made a more powerful statement, but as an author, understanding the situation, writing a book, I expect him to take himself and his readers more seriously and offer some Petersonesque advice.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11999292547434245613" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14209409033413186644">Hate the Nazis as much as you want, they did have a good nose for quality literature. Too bad they used that skill to burn books. This novel, in it's original shape (Fabian, Die Geschichte eines Moralisten) ended up in flames, even though it was already edited to make it salonfähig. The decadence and sexual exploits it described was at that time just too much to handle. The left-wing politics from Fabian/Kästner helped little to improve its image.<br/>Rather than taking the hint, Kästner decided to m</span>
  <span id="freeText14209409033413186644" style="display:none">Hate the Nazis as much as you want, they did have a good nose for quality literature. Too bad they used that skill to burn books. This novel, in it's original shape (Fabian, Die Geschichte eines Moralisten) ended up in flames, even though it was already edited to make it salonfähig. The decadence and sexual exploits it described was at that time just too much to handle. The left-wing politics from Fabian/Kästner helped little to improve its image.<br />Rather than taking the hint, Kästner decided to make the best out of the situation and opted for Inner Migration, instead of leaving the country like so many of his colleagues did. <br />After the war, Kästner rewrote the novel, tweaked it. The version I read is the so called uncensored one. I really like the book but the ending felt a bit rushed. Nevertheless I highly recommend this book. (and pretty much any other book that ended up in the bonfires from hell).</span>

    <a title="Charles Low" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/89277989-charles-low"><img alt="Charles Low" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546575746p2/89277989.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2683696927?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Charles Low" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Charles Low" href="/user/show/89277989-charles-low">Charles Low</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2683696927&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2683696927" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1679505343794218505">I read the nyrb classics edition that is translated from the German by Cyrus Brooks. I really enjoyed reading this book that tells the story of Jakub Fabian a moralist. He is such a passive character and perhaps a prototype for millennials today in that he's overeducated, in precarious unemployment that doesn't match what he wants and above all else seems a bit lost. I think the best moments of the story happen between Labude and Fabian, and unfortunately there was not enough of that friendship.</span>
  <span id="freeText1679505343794218505" style="display:none">I read the nyrb classics edition that is translated from the German by Cyrus Brooks. I really enjoyed reading this book that tells the story of Jakub Fabian a moralist. He is such a passive character and perhaps a prototype for millennials today in that he's overeducated, in precarious unemployment that doesn't match what he wants and above all else seems a bit lost. I think the best moments of the story happen between Labude and Fabian, and unfortunately there was not enough of that friendship. The story is quite short and covers a seemingly quite short period of Fabian's life, when it hits a downward spiral. While I enjoyed the novel, it lacks a bit of depth in plot and characterisation. But it is enjoyable and eerily prescient and similar in atmosphere and tone to something that could be written today.</span>
